[Atharvan (kс╣гudra).тАФcatu┼Ыcatv─Бriс╣Е┼Ыat. mantroktaskambh─Бdhy─Бtmadevatyam. tr─Бiс╣гс╣нubham*: 1. vir─Бс╕Н jagat─л; 2, 8. bhurij; 7, 13. paroс╣гс╣Зih; 10, 14, 16, 18, ig. upariс╣гс╣н─Бd bс╣Ыhat─л; 11, 12, 15, 20, 22, 39. upariс╣гс╣н─Бjjyotirjagat─л; 17. 3-av. 6-p. jagat─л; 21. bс╣Ыhat─лgarbh─Б тАЩnuс╣гс╣нubh; 23-30, 37, 40. anuс╣гс╣нubh; 31. madhyejyotirjagat─л; 32, 34, 36. upariс╣гс╣н─Бdvir─Бс╕Н bс╣Ыhat─л; 33. paravir─Бс╕Н anuс╣гс╣нubh; 35. 4-p. jagat─л; 38, 42, 43. triс╣гс╣нubh*; 41. ─Бrs─л 3-p. g─Бyatr─л; 44. ─Бrcy anuс╣гс╣нubh (?) тАа.]

Comment

Found also (except vss. 13, 42-44) in P─Бipp. xvii. (with slight differences of order, noted under the verses). Neither K─Бu┼Ы. nor V─Бit. takes any notice of the hymn.тАФ*тМКIf the hymn is tr─Бiс╣гс╣нubham, why are these verses specified? see note to 38.тМЛ тАатМКAs to the Anukr’s description of this verse, see under the verse.тМЛ

Translations

Translated: Muir, v. 380-384 (vss. 1-41); Ludwig, p. 400; Scherman, p. 50 (vss. 1-41: with comment); Deussen, Geschichte, i. 1. 310 (sympathetic interpretation and useful introduction); Henry, 22, 68; Griffith, ii. 26.тАФAs to the appearance of Brahm as a new conception, as a Wunderding (yakс╣г├б: vs. 38), cf. the Kena Upanishad, ┬з 3, vss. 14-25, and Deussen’s introduction, Sechzig Upanishad’s, p. 204.тАФThe hymn is nearly related to the following one (8), and, with many a riddle and paradox, they both lead up to the fundamental conception of the Upanishads (see last verse of hymn 8), the idea of the ─Аtman.тАФIn the new volume of the Ved. Stud., iii. 126 ff., Geldner discusses yakс╣г├б at length.

Whitney
Translation
  1. In what member of him is penance situated? in what member of him is
    right (с╣Ыt├б) deposited? where is situated [his] vow (vrat├б), where
    his faith? in what member of him is truth established?
Notes

Ppp. combines in a tapo тАШsya. In b, the pada-text reads
asya: ├бdhi: ─Б╠Бре░hitam. There is no reason for calling the verse vir─Бj.

  1. From what member of him flames Agni? from what member blows (

p┼л

) M─Бtari┼Ыvan? from what member doth the moon measure out, measuring the
member of great Skambha?

Skambha, lit. ‘prop, support, pillar,’ strangely used in this hymn as
frame of the universe or half-personified as its soul. Ppp. reads in
d skambhasya mahan mim-. тМКRead vim├нm─Бno in d?тМЛ

Griffith

Which of his members is the seat of Fervour: Which is the base of Ceremonial Order? Where in him standeth Faith? Where Holy Duty? Where, in what part of him is truth implanted?

Out of which member glows the light of Agni? Form which proceeds the breath of Matarisvan? From which doth Chandra measure out his journey, travelling over Skambha’s mighty body?

Whitney
Translation
  1. In what member of him is situated the earth? in what member is
    situated the atmosphere? in what member is the sky set? in what member
    is situated what is beyond the sky?
Notes

This is one of the five verses (3-6, 9) which are left by the Anukr. to
fall under the general description of the hymn as tr─Бiс╣гс╣нubham. All of
them are more or less redundant; this, for example, is as much bhurij
as vs. 2, which was so described. Ppp. puts the verse before our 2.

Griffith

Which of his members is the earth’s upholder? Which gives the middle air a base to rest on? Where, in which member is the sky established? Where hath the space above the sky its dwelling?

Griffith

Whitherward yearning blazeth Agni upward? Whitherward yearning bloweth Matarisvan? Who out of many, tell me, is that Skambha to whom with long- ing go the turning pathways?

Whitney
Translation
  1. By how much did Skambha enter the existent? how much of him lies
    along that which will exist? what one member he made thousand-fold, by
    how much did Skambha enter there?
Notes

The Anukr. again passes without notice the redundant syllable in c.

Griffith

How far within the past hath Skambha entered? How much of him hath reached into the future? That one part which he set in thousand places,–how far did Skambha penetrate within it?

  1. Where penance, striding forth, maintains the higher vow (vrat├б),

strangely named by the Anukr. тМКPpp. exchanges the places of vratam and
с╣Ыtam in b, c, and of ─Бpas and brahma in d.тМЛ

Who out of many, tell me, is that Skambha. In whom the Sages earliest born, the Richas, Saman, Yajus, Earth, and the one highest Sage abide?

Translation
  1. Where both immortality (amс╣Ы╠Бta) and death are set together in man
    (p├║ruс╣гa), of whom the ocean, the veins (n─Бс╕Н─л╠Б) are set together in
    man, that Skambha etc. etc.
Notes

Ppp. reads, for b, puruс╣гa┼Ы ca sam─Бhit─Бс╕е, and puts the verse after
our 16. Read in a mс╣Ыty├║┼Ы ca (an accent-sign slipped out of place).

Griffith

Who out of many, tell me, is the Skambha. Who comprehendeth, for mankind, both immortality and death, He who containeth for mankind the gathered waters as his veins?

резрем рдпрд╕реНрдп рдЪрддрд╕реНрд░рдГ ...{Loading}...
Whitney
Translation
  1. Of whom the four directions are (sth─Б) the teeming (? prapyas├б)
    veins, where the sacrifice hath strode forth, that Skambha etc. etc.
Notes

Pratham─Б╠Бs at end of b in our edition seems to be a misprint for
prathas─Б╠Бs, intended as a correction of prapyas─Б╠Бs, which last,
however, is distinctly read by all our mss.* (p. praре░pyas─Б╠Бс╕е); for the
formation, compare -bhyasa from root bh─л (through a secondary root
bhyas). *тМКExcept P., which has prathas─Б╠Бs; SPP. puts -m─Б╠Бs into his
text against his fifteen authorities, which give prapyas─Б╠Бs.

Griffith

Who out of many, tell me, is that Skambha, He whose chief arteries stand there, the sky’s four regions, he irk whom Sacrifice putteth forth its might?

Translation
  1. Whoever know the br├бhman in man, they know the most exalted one;
    whoever knows the most exalted one, and whoever knows Praj─Бpati, whoever
    know the chief br├бhmaс╣Зa, they know also accordingly (anu-sam-vid)
    the Skambha.
Notes

For both b and f, Ppp. reads te skambham arasaс╣Б viduс╕е
(intending anusaс╣Бviduс╕е?).

Griffith

They who in Purusha understand Brahma know Him who is. Supreme. He who knows Him who is Supreme, and he who knows the Lord of Life, These know the loftiest Power Divine, and thence know Skam- bha thoroughly.

Griffith

Who out of many, tell me, is that Skambha From whom they hewed the lichas off, from whom they chipped the Yajus, he Whose hairs are Sama-verses and his mouth the Atharvangi- rases?

Whitney
Translation
  1. The branch of the non-existent, standing forth, people know as in a
    manner the highest thing; also the lower ones who worship (upa-─Бs) thy
    branch think [it?] the existent thing.
Notes

The translation of .this highly obscure verse is only mechanical, and as
literal as possible. Ppp. has only the first half. The definition of the
Anukr. is a strange one; the verse is only a bhurig anuс╣гс╣нubh (in
virtue of the iva, which properly is to be reduced to va, making a
regular anuс╣гс╣нubh).

Griffith

Men count as ’twere a thing supreme nonentity’s conspicuous branch; And lower man who serve thy branch regard it as an entity.

Whitney
Translation
  1. People know the golden-embryo [as] highest, not to be overcrowed
    (anatyudy├б); the skambh├б in the beginning poured forth that gold
    within the world.
Notes

Ppp. puts this verse after our 30.

Griffith

Men know Hiranyagarbha as supreme and inexpressible: In the beginning, in the midst of the world, Skambha poured that gold.

Whitney
Translation
  1. In the skambh├б the worlds, in the skambh├б penance, in the
    skambh├б right is set; thee, O skambh├б, I know plainly [as] set all
    together in Indra.
Notes

The mss. are much at variance in regard to sk├бmbha in c; all save
W. (the poorest and least trustworthy of all) end the word with m, and
O.s.m.D. accent skambh├бm. That sk├бmbha is really intended can hardly
admit of question; Ppp. appears to read it.

Griffith

Translation

c, but where the whole sense is so mystically obscure alterations
help little.

  1. Name with name he calls aloud, before the sun, before the dawn; as
    first the goat (? aj├б) came into being, he went unto that autocracy
    beyond which there is nothing else existent.
Notes

Ppp. reads johav─лmi in a, and jag─Бma (for iy─Бya) in d. The
translators all understand aj├бs here as ’the unborn one,’ and with
more reason than in most places elsewhere. The description given by the
Anukr. of the very irregular verse (8 + 8: 10 + 10 + 11 = 47) is
altogether ill-fitting.

Griffith

Ere sun and dawn man calls and calls one Deity by the other’s name. When the Unborn first sprang into existence he reached that independent sovran lordship; than which aught higher never hath arisen.

рдореВрд▓рдореН (VS)

рдпрд╕реНрдпреТ рднреВрдорд┐рдГреС рдкреНрд░реТрдорд╛рдиреНрддрд░рд┐реСрдХреНрд╖рдореБреТрддреЛрджрд░реСрдореНред
рджрд┐рд╡рдВреТ рдпрд╢реНрдЪреТрдХреНрд░реЗ рдореВреТрд░реНрдзрд╛рдирдВреТ рддрд╕реНрдореИреС рдЬреНрдпреЗреТрд╖реНрдард╛рдпреТ рдмреНрд░рд╣реНрдореСрдгреЗреТ рдирдордГреС рее

рейреи рдпрд╕реНрдп рднреВрдорд┐рдГ ...{Loading}...
Whitney
Translation
  1. Of whom earth is model (pram─Б╠Б) and atmosphere belly; who made the
    sky his headтАФto that chief br├бhman be homage.
Notes

In this and the two following verses and vs. 36 we have the anomaly that
br├бhman, neuter, is apparently referred to by the masculine relative
y├бs (in accordance with which the genitive y├бsya is also doubtless
to be understood as masculine); perhaps we ought to render the last p─Бda
thus: ’to him, [who is] the chief br├бhman,’ etc. тМКCf. Deussen, p.
312.тМЛ The verse is shorter by two syllables than verses 34 and 36, with
which the Anukr. reckons it, and, on the other hand, agrees with 33, to
which the Anukr. gives a different name.

Griffith

Be reverence paid to him, that highest Brahma, whose base is Earth, his belly Air, who made the sky to be his head.

рдкрдгреНрдбрд┐рдд рдХреНрд╖реЗрдордХрд░рдгрджрд╛рд╕ рддреНрд░рд┐рд╡реЗрджреА - рдкрджрд╛рд░реНрдердГ

рдкрджрд╛рд░реНрдерд╛рдиреНрд╡рдпрднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдГ - (рднреВрдорд┐рдГ) рднреВрдорд┐ (рдпрд╕реНрдп) рдЬрд┐рд╕ [рдкрд░рдореЗрд╢реНрд╡рд░] рдХреЗ (рдкреНрд░рдорд╛) рдкрд╛рджрдореВрд▓ [рдХреЗ рд╕рдорд╛рди] (рдЙрдд) рдФрд░ (рдЕрдиреНрддрд░рд┐рдХреНрд╖рдореН) рдЕрдиреНрддрд░рд┐рдХреНрд╖ [рдкреГрдерд┐рд╡реА рдФрд░ рд╕реВрд░реНрдп рдХреЗ рдмреАрдЪ рдХрд╛ рдЖрдХрд╛рд╢] (рдЙрджрд░рдореН) рдЙрджрд░ [рд╕рдорд╛рди] рд╣реИред (рджрд┐рд╡рдореН) рд╕реВрд░реНрдп рдХреЛ (рдпрдГ) рдЬрд┐рд╕рдиреЗ (рдореВрд░реНрдзрд╛рдирдореН) рдорд╕реНрддрдХ [рд╕рдорд╛рди] (рдЪрдХреНрд░реЗ) рд░рдЪрд╛, (рддрд╕реНрдореИ) рдЙрд╕ (рдЬреНрдпреЗрд╖реНрдард╛рдп) рдЬреНрдпреЗрд╖реНрда [рд╕рдм рд╕реЗ рдмрдбрд╝реЗ рд╡рд╛ рд╕рдм рд╕реЗ рд╢реНрд░реЗрд╖реНрда] (рдмреНрд░рд╣реНрдордгреЗ) рдмреНрд░рд╣реНрдо [рдкрд░рдорд╛рддреНрдорд╛] рдХреЛ (рдирдордГ) рдирдорд╕реНрдХрд╛рд░ рд╣реИ реерейреирее

рдкрдгреНрдбрд┐рдд рдХреНрд╖реЗрдордХрд░рдгрджрд╛рд╕ рддреНрд░рд┐рд╡реЗрджреА - рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдердГ

Whitney
Translation
  1. The skambh├б sustains both heaven-and-earth here; the skambh├б
    sustains the wide atmosphere; the skambh├б sustains the six wide
    directions; into the skambh├б hath entered this whole existence
    (bh├║vana).
Notes

The pada-text has (as translated) skambh├й in d. Ppp. puts the
verse after our 36, and reads in a pс╣Ыthiv─лс╣Б dy─Бm ut─Б тАЩm┼лс╣Б, and in
d combines skambh─Бi тАЩdaс╣Б. The Anukr. takes no notice of the
irregularity of the verse (14 + 11: 11 + 13 = 49). тМКBergaigne, Rel.
V├йd., ii. 122, would separate pradi┼Ыas from urv─лs.тМЛ

Griffith

Skambha set fast these two, the earth and heaven, Skambha maintained the ample air between them. Skambha established the six spacious regions: this whole world Skambha entered and pervaded.

    strode (? kr─Бnt├б) in penance on the back of the seaтАФin it are set
    (┼Ыri) whatever gods there are, like the branches of a tree roundabout
    the trunk.
Notes

The first p─Бda is repeated below, as 8. 15 c. Ppp. combines in d
to parit─Бi тАЩva. Notwithstanding the lack of a syllable in a, the
Anukr. тМКbalancing a with redundant d?тМЛ calls the verse simply a
triс╣гс╣нubh; the hymn is so long that it has apparently been forgotten
that the whole was called tr─Бiс╣гс╣нubha, and that therefore no triс╣гс╣нubh
needs a further specification. Since there are more regular
anuс╣гс╣нubh-verses than triс╣гс╣нubh also, we should expect rather the
designation ─Бnuс╣гс╣нubham for the hymn. Read at end of a m├бdhye (an
accent-sign dropped out). тМКWith regard to Brahm as a “wonder” (yakс╣г├б),
see introduction.тМЛ

Griffith

Absorbed in Fervour, is the mighty Being, in the world’s centre, on the waters’ surface. To him the Deities, one and all betake them. So stand the tree- trunk with the branches round it.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Unto which with the two hands, with the two feet, with speech, with
    hearing, with sight; unto which the gods continually render (pra-yam)
    tribute, unmeasured in the measured outтАФthat skambh├б tell [me]:
    which forsooth is he?
Notes

Notwithstanding the discordance of case, v├нmite is perhaps coordinate
with y├бsm─Бi. Ppp. omits the first two p─Бdas. The dual and the
repetition of y├бsm─Бi make it probable that we have to supply in them
‘one renders tribute,’ or the like. The Anukr. takes no notice of the
redundant syllable in d.

Griffith

Who out of many, tell me, is that Skambha. To whom the Deities with hands, with feet, and voice, and ear, and eye. Present unmeasured tribute in the measured hall of sacrifice?

Whitney
Translation
  1. A certain pair of maidens, of diverse form, weave, betaking
    themselves to it, the six-pegged web; the one draws forth the threads
    (t├бntu), the other sets [them]; they wrest not off (apa-vс╣Ыj), they
    go not to an end.

Griffith

Singly the two young Maids of different colours approach the six-pegged warp in turns and weave it. The one draws out the threads, the other lays them: they break them not, they reach no end of labour.

рдкрдгреНрдбрд┐рдд рдХреНрд╖реЗрдордХрд░рдгрджрд╛рд╕ рддреНрд░рд┐рд╡реЗрджреА - рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдердГ

Translation
  1. Of them, as of two women dancing about, I do not distinguish
    (vi-j├▒─Б) which is beyond; a man (p├║m─Бс╣Еs), weaves it, ties [it] up;
    a man hath borne it about upon the firmament (n─Б╠Бka).

Griffith

Of these two, dancing round as ’twere, I cannot distinguish whether ranks before the other. A Male in weaves this web, a Male divides it: a Male hath stretched it to the cope of heaven

Translation
  1. These pegs propped up the sky; the chants they made shuttles for
    weaving.

Griffith

These pegs have buttressed up the sky. The Samans have turned them into shuttles for the weaving.
